        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546"><meta property="og:title" content="First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na2FePO4F with Strong Antisite Disorder"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.3c02546.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Polyanionic Na2FePO4F is one of the most important cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. The orthorhombic β-Na2FePO4F material has been studied extensively and intensively since it was proposed. In this article, a novel monoclinic sodium phosphate fluoride α-Na2FePO4F is concerned. Kirsanova’s experiment showed that Na and Fe ions in α-Na2FePO4F are prone to antisite, leading to strong antisite disorder. Through first-principle calculations, we show that the steric effect, the magnetic exchange and superexchange interactions between transition-metal cations are shown to be the main driving forces for Na+/Fe2+ antisite disorder. We first calculated the crystal structures, electronic properties, and cohesive energies of all the 10 antisite phases of α-Na2FePO4F and β-Na2FePO4F. Then, we compared the difference charge densities, magnetism, binding energies, and electrostatic potentials of α-Na2FePO4F and β-Na2FePO4F materials in the antisite and pristine phases. In α-Na2FePO4F, the binding energy of the antisite phase with the lowest binding energy is almost degenerate with that of the pristine phase. Moreover, only small differences of the electrostatic potential and the charge density distribution are found between the antisite (with lowest energy) and the pristine phases of α-Na2FePO4F, which also helped elaborate the facile formation of Na+/Fe2+ antisite in the α-Na2FePO4F material. Our research contributes to the understanding of the mechanism of Na+/Fe2+ antisite and the development of high-performance polyanionic cathode materials."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.3c02546.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187d583bffd404-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187d583bffd404-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '6b492e16-4281-49d9-93b7-790ed35e5911'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187d583bffd404-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'inorganic_chemistry';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});

<script nonce="93187d583bffd404-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "inocaj";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "First-Principles Studies on the Structural and Electronic Properties of α-Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;FePO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;F with Strong Antisite Disorder", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">4</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0011.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Polyanionic Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is one of the most important cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. The orthorhombic β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material has been studied extensively and intensively since it was proposed. In this article, a novel monoclinic sodium phosphate fluoride α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is concerned. Kirsanova’s experiment showed that Na and Fe ions in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are prone to antisite, leading to strong antisite disorder. Through first-principle calculations, we show that the steric effect, the magnetic exchange and superexchange interactions between transition-metal cations are shown to be the main driving forces for Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder. We first calculated the crystal structures, electronic properties, and cohesive energies of all the 10 antisite phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Then, we compared the difference charge densities, magnetism, binding energies, and electrostatic potentials of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials in the antisite and pristine phases. In α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, the binding energy of the antisite phase with the lowest binding energy is almost degenerate with that of the pristine phase. Moreover, only small differences of the electrostatic potential and the charge density distribution are found between the antisite (with lowest energy) and the pristine phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, which also helped elaborate the facile formation of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material. Our research contributes to the understanding of the mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite and the development of high-performance polyanionic cathode materials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D15300%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJingjin%2BChen%252C%2BLi-Hong%2BZhang%252C%2BZhishuo%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D62%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacs.inorgchem.3c02546%26title%3DFirst-Principles%2BStudies%2Bon%2Bthe%2BStructural%2Band%2BElectronic%2BProperties%2Bof%2B%25CE%25B1-Na2FePO4F%2Bwith%2BStrong%2BAntisite%2BDisorder%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0020-1669%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dinocaj%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D15309%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure"> Chemical structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290982&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diseases and disorders"> Diseases and disorders </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290898&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Energy"> Energy </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="hlFld-Abstract synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><span><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Experiment has shown that Na and Fe ions in a novel monoclinic sodium phosphate fluoride, namely, α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, are prone to antisite, leading to strong antisite disorder. The steric effect and the magnetic exchange and superexchange interactions between transition metal cations are shown to be the main driving forces for Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder through first-principles calculations.</p></span></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have received a great deal of attention due to their relatively long cycle life, high theoretical capacity, and desirable rate capability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> However, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have advantages in resources and cost, so it has great potential to become a substitute for LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> The energy storage capacity of SIBs depends greatly on the cathode material. Cathodes can affect the power density of SIBs by influencing the ability to accommodate sodium ions and the smoothness of the transport channels. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> Moreover, the depletion of the Na<sup>+</sup> active material and the impurity content also affect the lifetime of the battery. Lastly, the Na<sup>+</sup> and high redox potential of SIBs will play a crucial role in determining the operating voltage and reversible capacity of the battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> Therefore, the development of high-performance cathode materials (e.g., high redox potential, fast charge transfer and high capacity) is an important part of current SIB research. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Currently, cathode materials of SIBs mainly include transition-metal oxides, polyanionic compounds, Prussian blue derivatives, and organic compounds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The actual specific capacity of these cathode materials is 120–200 mAh/g, and the voltage range is between 2.6 and 3.2 V. Among the known sodium storage electrode materials, polyanionic materials have a good prospect because of their stable structure, high safety, and open sodium-ion transport channels. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 17 to 20">(17−20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Phosphate fluoride cathode materials introduce highly electronegative fluorine atoms into the polyanionic structure, thereby increasing the redox pair voltage and the energy density of the cathode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> In 2007, Ellis et al. reported on a sodium iron phosphate of the Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material with an orthorhombic phase (which is named β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F) with a space group of <i>Pbcn</i>, whose physical and electrochemical properties as a novel cathode material for SIBs have been extensively studied. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> However, the poor intrinsic conductivity and limited active sodium sites of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F make its rate capability and reversible capacity unsatisfactory. Huang et al. proposed a doping strategy represented by Cu<sup>2+</sup> to improve the electrochemical performance, and the prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.95</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C composites showed better electrochemical performance than the previously reported Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Jin et al. found that the average voltage could be increased, and the band gap and the Na ion diffusion barrier could be reduced by co-doping four metal elements (Ti, Co, Ni, and Sn). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Kacemi et al. found that Mn doping could significantly increase the conductivity and voltage of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, resulting in a high-energy density SIB cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The diffusion channel for Na<sup>+</sup> is not blocked and Na<sup>+</sup> tends to migrate along the <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> planes in the two-dimensional framework with low migration barriers; therefore, β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is considered to be a good cathode material for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 25 to 28">(25−28)</a> In 2020, Kirsanova et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> first reported a novel monoclinic Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F with a space group of <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i> named as α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. In contrast to β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, there are few experimental and theoretical studies on the physical and electrochemical properties of the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase. Because the space groups of α- and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials are not the same, there are also large differences in their crystal structures. Experiment and DFT calculations show that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure is 2 orders of magnitude higher than that in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure. Relative to β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F has a lower Na<sup>+</sup> migration barrier along the main diffusion path parallel to the <i>b</i> axis, and there are additional diffusion bypasses allowing Na<sup>+</sup> cations to bypass. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Therefore, it is of great significance to study the physical properties of monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F synthesized via a hydrothermal method demonstrates strong antisite disorder between the Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Although the structural motif of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is similar to that of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, the β-Na2FePO4F shows no antisite disorder. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> The antisite disorder in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F corresponds to the exchange of some sodium ions at position 3a with transition metal (TM) ions at position 3b. In previous studies, it was found that the site exchange between TM and Na ions may have an important influence on the electrochemical properties of the cathode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 33 to 36">(33−36)</a> For example, the presence of TM ions in the Na layer will reduce the space of the Na layer and thus decreases the diffusion coefficient of Na ions. The TM ions in the Na ion layer will also gradually migrate to the particle surface, leading to the TM loss and making the structure unstable, also resulting in voltage reduction and capacity fading of the cathode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 29 and 37 to 39">(29,37−39)</a> The antisite can also cause obvious anisotropic stresses and affect the structural stability of the cathode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> However, some studies have shown that a certain degree of antisite disorder can alleviate the contraction of the interlayer distance in cathode materials at high charged states, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 to 44">(42−44)</a> which is beneficial for the structural stability during the electrochemical cycling. Therefore, it is very important to understand the antisite disorder of TM and Na ions as well as the modulation of this antisite disorder in order to improve the performance of electrode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">Experiment has shown that Na and Fe ions in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are prone to antisite, leading to strong antisite disorder. The main drawback of cation disorder is the irreversible phase evolution and the loss of Na<sup>+</sup> active sites that deteriorates the battery capacity and cycle stability of active materials. The understanding of the antisite will help develop high-performance polyanionic cathodes. For example, some measures can then be made to control the antisites disorders: (i) suppressing the driving force of antisite from the steric effect by ion exchange (e.g., Na/Li exchange), (ii) tuning the magnetic interactions by TM cationic substitution (i.e., the magnetic interaction is adjusted), and (iii) kinetically controlling the TM migration by tuning the thermodynamic and kinetic conditions and surface reactions during synthesis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> On the other hand, antisite TM ions in the surface layer could restrict the H2/H3 phase transformation in the surface region, which diminishes the propagation of undesirable phase transformations from the surface to bulk. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, we focus on the occurrence of strong antisite disorder in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase and discuss the physical mechanism of the strong antisite disorder. Using ab initio approaches, the structural and electronic properties of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are calculated. The analysis shows that steric effects, the magnetic exchange and superexchange interactions between transition-metal cations are the main driving forces for Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder. We first calculated the structures and cohesive energies of all the 10 antisite phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Then, we calculated and compared the charge densities, band structures, magnetism, binding energies, and electrostatic potentials of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials between the antisite phase and the pristine phase in order to further elaborate the physical mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite. Our calculations show that the binding energy of the antisite phase (the one with the lowest cohesive energy among the 10 antisite phases) is essentially degenerate with the binding energy of the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Moreover, only small differences in the electrostatic potential and charge density distribution are found between the antisite phase and the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Furthermore, we found that, for the same antisite phase in β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, the changes in the cohesive energy, electrostatic potential, and charge density distribution between the antisite and the pristine phases are more obvious than those in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. All these results indicate that the antisite disorder is more easily formed in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F than in β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Computational Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">All the first-principles calculations were performed using the VASP and RESCU software packages, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> which are based on the spin-polarized density functional theory (DFT). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> VASP is based on the plane-wave basis and the projector augmented wave (PAW) representation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a> while RESCU is based on the basis of atomic orbitals and is suitable for large-scale calculations. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The electron exchange-correlation interaction was parameterized by the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) with the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> To deal with the strong correlation character of 3d electrons in the transition-metal Fe ions, the GGA + <i>U</i> method is adopted. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 54 to 56">(54−56)</a> An effective <i>U</i><sub>eff</sub> = <i>U</i> – <i>J</i> of 4.0 eV is adopted for Fe, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> following previous studies where good performance was achieved in the calculations of cathode materials. Magnetic ordering is studied by the spin-polarized DFT calculations in all the systems. A plane-wave kinetic energy cut-off of 520 eV is used for all the VASP calculations, and the electronic energy convergence is 10<sup>–6</sup> eV. The crystal structures are completely relaxed until the Hellmann–Feynman force on each ion is converged to be less than 0.01 eV Å<sup>–1</sup>. A 2 × 2 × 1 supercell containing 72 atoms is employed to model the structures of α- and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Brillouin zone integrations are approximated by special <i>k</i>-point sampling of the Monkhorst–Pack scheme <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> with 2 × 5 × 2 grids for both the α- and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F systems. We have tested the convergence of both the <i>k</i>-point mesh and the plane-wave kinetic energy cutoff (<i>E</i><sub>Ncut</sub>) in our calculations. When the cohesive energy meets the convergence standard of 1 meV, the converged values of <i>k</i>-mesh and <i>E</i><sub>Ncut</sub> are obtained and then adopted in the calculations.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">3.1.  Crystal Structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F Phases</h3><div class="NLM_p">At present, there are two phases of Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, namely, the orthorhombic β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref58" aria-label="reference 30 to 32 and 58">(30−32,58)</a> and the monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> whose space groups are <i>Pbcn</i> and <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i>, respectively. The top and side views of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d. Na occupies two distinct positions in β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, i.e., Na1 and Na2, between the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra and the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. The [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedron is formed by the coordination of four oxygen ions and two fluorine ions, and two [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra form the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra through the face-sharing of F–O–F along the <i>a</i> direction. The [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra then build up the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chain through the corner-sharing of F ions. Two unique sites of Na in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure have similar coordination environments; however, the Na2 atom has a more compact atomic environment than that of Na1 because the Na2 atom is close to the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chain and the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra, which squeezes the atomic environment of Na2. More discussion is presented later. For the monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b, although the motifs are similar to those of orthorhombic β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, its structural properties are significantly different from those of the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. The Na ions in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, which are surrounded by [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra and [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra, extend alternately along the <i>ac</i> plane. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> As with β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, the two [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra are linked through the corner-sharing of F ions to form the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chain along the <i>b</i> axis. The [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains are interconnected via [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra with the Na ions located in the interstitial positions of the framework. As can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,c, generally speaking, the atomic environments of the four sodium ions in β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are similar; all the Na ions are surrounded by four [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedrons and one [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Top and side views of the crystal structures of (a, b) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and (c, d) β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Although the structural features of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are similar, the orientations of the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra as well as the direction of connection of the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains are very different in the two structures. There are two [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains labeled as chains 1 and 2 in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,d, respectively. The black lines in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,d indicate the orientation of the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra. In the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure, chains 1 and 2 are aligned in opposite directions, whereas in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure, the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra chains (i.e., chains 1 and 2) are oriented in the same direction. The spatial orientation of the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra is also different in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structures. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,c, each red circle contains four sodium ions, six [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra, and one [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra. In the orthorhombic β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), half of the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra are facing upward and the other half are facing downward in the red circle. However, in the monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a), one pattern is that the red circle has four [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra facing upward and two [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra facing downward. Another pattern is that there are two [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra facing up and four [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra facing down.</div><div class="NLM_p">The structural parameters and cohesive energies of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F calculated here are compared with the existing theoretical and experimental results, as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. The theoretical lattice parameters are slightly larger than the experimental data, and the deviation is reasonable. In terms of volume expansion per unit cell (with eight formula units), α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F showed a difference of 33.73 and 34.53 Å<sup>3</sup>, respectively, from the previously optimized cell volumes, both deviating from the experimental values by approximately 4%. Overall, our calculated lattice parameters, space groups, and unit cell volumes of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F agree well with experimental and previous theoretical calculations. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref59" aria-label="reference 26 and 59">(26,59)</a> The ground-state magnetism for both the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structures is found to be ferromagnetic, which is also in good agreement with previous experimental and theoretical results. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> More discussion on the magnetic properties of the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is presented later.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">3.2.  Antisite Disorder in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F Phase</h3><div class="NLM_p">Kirsanova et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> found that there is a strong antisite disorder between Na and Fe ions in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material. However, for the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase, the phenomenon of antisite disorder has not been found in previous experimental and theoretical studies. There are four pristine sodium sites (denoted as Na<sub>1</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>4</sub>) and two pristine iron sites (denoted as Fe<sub>5</sub> and Fe<sub>6</sub>); see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>. According to the experiment of Kirsanova et al., <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> three of the four pristine sodium sites and both of the two pristine iron sites in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F can be jointly occupied by Na and Fe ions (that is, each site is occupied by Na or Fe with a certain probability, and the occupancy is shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Experiment shows that the Na<sub>3</sub> site is always occupied by Na atoms (i.e., its occupancy = 1). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Among the other five sites (Na<sub>1</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>5</sub>, and Fe<sub>6</sub>), three sites are definitely occupied by Na atoms and two sites are completely occupied by Fe atoms. According to the occupancy given in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a>, we can clearly deduce that the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F has 10 and only 10 structures (including all possible antisite disorder structures in the experiment); see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>. The disorder structures are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> (also shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/suppl_file/ic3c02546_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) with the site occupancy shown with a “shadow” on each atomic sphere. These 10 structures (with or without antisites) are named as follows: Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>y</i></sub> gives a structure where two Fe atoms occupy the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> sites and the remaining three “disordered” sites are all occupied by Na atoms (see also <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Disordered structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F (occupancy is shown by a “shadow” on the atomic sphere).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. cohesive energy difference between the antisite and the pristine structures per formula unit.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The lattice constants, cohesive energies, cohesive energy differences, and magnetism were calculated for all the 10 structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub> have the same cohesive energy due to the symmetry and periodicity of their crystal structures. In addition, the results in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a> show that Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub> structures have the lowest cohesive energy, so Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> or Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub> structures can be regarded as the pristine structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F (i.e., without antisite). The other eight structures can be viewed as antisite phases. All the atomic positions in the unit cell for all the 10 structures are fully relaxed when performing first-principles calculations. The results show that the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra remain very stable. However, the orientation of the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra and the way in which the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra form chains are not the same in the different antisite structures. These results show that the antisite structures can also exist stably. The structural characteristics of these antisite phases mainly include the following: (1) two [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra first form the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra through face-sharing, and then [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] forms [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F/O–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains through corner-sharing of F/O, such as in the structures of Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>, Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub>, and Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>; (2) the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra form [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>]–O–[FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] chains through corner-sharing of O atoms in the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra, such as in the structures of Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub>, and Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>; and (3) two [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra first form the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra through edge-sharing, and the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] form [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–O–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains through O-sharing, such as the structure of Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>. Such three types of chains form three-dimensional frameworks through corner-sharing of O atoms in the [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra. In these antisite phases, the cohesive energy difference between Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> and the pristine Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> is very small (&lt;0.003 eV/f.u.), indicating that Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite should easily occur. The cohesive energies of the other antisite phases are also larger than that of the pristine phase (however, the differences are small), which indicates that the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material is prone to Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite formation from the perspective of energy. Since Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> is the structure with the lowest energy among the antisite structures, we will mainly focus on the properties of this antisite structure in the following discussion. Unless otherwise noted, the antisite phase in the following text then refers to this Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">The top and side views of the α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> structure are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,b, which has the lowest cohesive energy among the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F antisite structures. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d shows the antisite structure of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, that is, the β-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> structure, where the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite is artificially made to be the same way as α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. For the antisite phase β-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra is destroyed after full structural relaxation, and the binding energy of this antisite structure is quite different from that of the pristine structure (approximately 0.48 eV/f.u.), indicating that the similar Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite formation is not possible in β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. According to the band structures (given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>), both the pristine α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structures have ferromagnetic ground states, while the two Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> phases in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F with the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite structures are antiferromagnetic (see also <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/suppl_file/ic3c02546_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). In addition, all these structures are semiconductors with wide band gaps. For all the structures mentioned here, it is common that the conduction band minimum (CBM) is mainly formed by Fe 3d states with a small contribution from O 2p states, while the valence band maximum (VBM) is dominated by Fe 3d states in hybridization with O 2p states.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Top views of (a) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F) and (b) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F). Side views of (c) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F) and (d) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Band structures of (a) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, (b) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F), (c) β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, and (d) Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.3.  Mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> Antisite in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F</h3><div class="NLM_p">The ionic radii of Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F structure are <i>r</i> (Na<sup>+</sup>) = 1.02 Å and <i>r</i> (Fe<sup>2+</sup>) = 0.78 Å, respectively; their ionic radii differ by 0.24 Å. This shows a relatively large difference in the radius between Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>. In the pristine structure (i.e., α-Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>), as mentioned earlier, two [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra first form the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra through face-sharing, and then the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra form [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>]–F–[Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] chains through corner-sharing of F<sup>–</sup> (shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>). In the case of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite, the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra form a three-dimensional frame by means of corner-sharing of F<sup>–</sup> ions (see also <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>). Moreover, the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra with the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite and without the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite all have relatively small distortions. Therefore, in this material, the steric effect for the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite is due to the “rearrangement” of the [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra in the antisite structure. It is necessary to point out that Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder can easily happen during the synthesis process in the monoclinic α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra arrangements in pristine α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and in α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase, the distance between the Fe<sub>5</sub>–Fe<sub>6</sub> atoms is 3.01 Å, while in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase, the distance between the Fe<sub>5</sub>–Fe<sub>6</sub> atoms is 3.35 Å. The distance between Fe<sub>5</sub>–Fe<sub>6</sub> atoms plays an important role in the structural stability of the material (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>), because the shorter the Fe<sub>5</sub>–Fe<sub>6</sub> bond, the stronger the bond and the more stable the structure. Thus, the shorter bond length of Fe<sub>5</sub>–Fe<sub>6</sub> in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase indicates that β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is more stable than α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, which indicates that it is more difficult for antistatic disorder to occur in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase.</div><div class="NLM_p">The spin-polarization effect has a very important influence on the physical properties of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials. We calculated the cohesive energies of all the 10 antisite phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F with spin-polarization and without spin-polarization effects. The comparison of the cohesive energies is shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">Table 4</a>. The results show that, when spin polarization is not taken into account, the cohesive energy difference between the antisite phase α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (with the lowest cohesive energy among the antisite phases) and the pristine structure α-Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> is 0.134 eV/f.u. If spin-polarization effects are taken into account, this difference is greatly reduced to 0.003 eV/f.u. Therefore, it can be seen that the spin-polarization effect plays a very important role in the calculation of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl4"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 4. Cohesive Energies (per f.u.) Calculated with Spin-Polarization and without Spin-Polarization Effects for the Pristine and Antisite Structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col char=".">Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>5</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">with spin</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.800</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.800</td><td
