rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.739</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.601</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.599</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.502</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.381</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">without spin</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.510</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.510</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.376</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.352</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.365</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.330</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.130</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.130</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–53.103</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–52.947</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Our study shows that the driving force for the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite is not only from the steric effect but also from the direct exchange and superexchange between Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> magnetic ions, which is similar to the studies for Li(Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub><i>y</i></sub>Co<sub><i>z</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (NMC, <i>x</i> + <i>y</i> + <i>z</i> = 1) by Zheng et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> In the pristine α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), two adjacent [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra form [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra, and the distance between Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra is relatively close (approximately 3.40 Å), so there is a strong direct ferromagnetic exchange between these two Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions. However, the nearest Fe<sup>2+</sup>–Fe<sup>2+</sup> distance between two [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra is quite large (approximately 5.05 Å), so there is only a very weak superexchange between [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra. Considering both the strong direct exchange interactions between the Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra and the weak superexchange interactions between two [Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] double octahedra, a ferromagnetic ground state for the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase can be expected. In the antisite-phase α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>, the distribution of Fe<sup>2+</sup> tends to be more uniform (compared with the pristine phase). The distance between Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions in their [FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub>] octahedra is approximately 4.30 Å, so there is a relatively strong superexchange between Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions and their direct exchange interactions could be neglected, which eventually leads to antiferromagnetism of the α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> phase according to Anderson’s superexchange interaction theory. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> In fact, in the antisite-phase α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>, the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbitals of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and the p orbitals of F<sup>–</sup> form a strong σ bond. Because of this strong σ bond, the “linear” (approximately 180°; see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b) Fe<sup>2+</sup>–F<sup>–</sup>–Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> phase structure has a strong superexchange interaction. Our calculations for pristine α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F (with mainly direct exchange interactions) and antisite α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (with mainly superexchange interactions) ultimately lead to the conclusion that the pristine and the antisite phases have almost the same cohesive energy (differing by only 0.003 eV/f.u.), and therefore magnetic exchange and superexchange play an important role in the antisite disorder of this material.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Fe<sup>2+</sup>–Fe<sup>2+</sup> connections through O<sup>2–</sup> or F<sup>–</sup> ions in (a) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and (b) α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Next, we investigate the changes in the cohesive energy, electrostatic potential, and charge density distribution caused by the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite in order to better understand why the antisite disorder is more likely to occur in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase than in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase. The electronic structures (band structures, DOS, charge density, etc.) can be easily obtained from theoretical calculations, but they are difficult to obtain even with enough high precision in experiments. This makes it difficult to compare theoretical calculations with experimentation.</div><div class="NLM_p">First, the cohesive energies of the antisite structure (i.e., Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) and the pristine structure (i.e., Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) for α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials are shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl5" href="#tbl5">Table 5</a>, respectively. The cohesive energy of the α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> antisite phase is essentially degenerate with the cohesive energy of the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. The cohesive energy difference between the α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> antisite phase and the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is only 0.003 eV/f.u., and therefore, the antisite disorder will easily occur in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F phase (from the respect of cohesive energy). In contrast, the cohesive energy difference between the antisite phase of β-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> (i.e., an artificially hypothesized structure) and the pristine phase of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F is 0.042 eV/f.u., which is more than 10 times that of the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material. Therefore, it is much easier to have Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material than in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl5"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 5. Comparison of Cohesive Energies of Antisite (Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) and Pristine (Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) Phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F; Δ = <i>E</i>(Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) – <i>E</i>(Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>)</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">structures</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">cohesive energy (eV/f.u.)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">magnetism</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Δ (eV/f.u.)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">α-Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.800</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">FM</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.797</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">AFM</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.003</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">β-Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.836</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">FM</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">β-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–55.794</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">AFM</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.042</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> shows the differences of the electrostatic potential between the antisite structure (i.e., Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) and the pristine structure (i.e., Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) for α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials, respectively. For clarity, the electrostatic potential differences around Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions are also shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>. It can be seen that, around the Na<sup>+</sup> ion, the electrostatic potential difference between the antisite and pristine phases of the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material is significantly smaller than that of the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material (please pay special attention to the values marked in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b,e). The same conclusion can be obtained for the electrostatic potential difference near the Fe ion, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c,f. Thus, this also indicates that the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material is more prone to Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder than the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Electrostatic potential differences between antisite and pristine structures for (a) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and (d) β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, respectively. For clarity, the electrostatic potential differences around Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions are shown for (b, c) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and (e, f) β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The differences of electronic charge density between the antisite (i.e., Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) and the pristine structures (i.e., Fe<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub>) for α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>, respectively. In order to obtain a meaningful difference in charge densities, the atomic positions in the antisite phase are not relaxed but remain in the same atomic positions as the pristine phase (except for the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite). The charge density difference between the antisite phase and the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material is smaller than that of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, which can be seen also from the values marked in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>. Thus, the change in the charge densities also suggests that the Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite is more likely to occur in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material than in the β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/medium/ic3c02546_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Charge density differences between antisite and pristine structures for (a) α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and (b) β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/asset/images/large/ic3c02546_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this article, we have looked at a new monoclinic sodium phosphate fluoride, that is, α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Previous experiments have shown that Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions in α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F are prone to antisite formation, leading to strong antisite disorder in this material. Using first-principles approaches, the structural and electronic properties of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials have been calculated. The results show that the steric effect, the magnetic direct exchange and superexchange interactions between transition metal cations are the main driving forces for Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite disorder. We first calculated the structures and cohesive energies of all the 10 antisite phases of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. We have also calculated and compared the charge densities, magnetism, cohesive energies, and electrostatic potentials of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F materials between the antisite and pristine structures. Our calculations show that the binding energy (per formula unit) of the antisite phase (the one with the lowest cohesive energy among 10 antisite phases) is essentially degenerate with the binding energy of the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. Furthermore, the differences in the electrostatic potential and the charge density distribution are found to be fairly small between the antisite phase α-Fe<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>6</sub> and the pristine phase of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F. However, the changes in the cohesive energy, the electrostatic potential, and the charge density distribution are much more pronounced between antisite and pristine phases of β-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, suggesting an easier formation of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite in the α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F material. Our research is helpful to understand the mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> antisite and develop high-performance polyanionic cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Ten disordered antisite structures of α-Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F before and after atomic relaxations by first-principles methods (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/suppl_file/ic3c02546_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546/suppl_file/ic3c02546_si_001.pdf">ic3c02546_si_001.pdf (415.66 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02546" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>71</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">258</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">265</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2012.03.161</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D71%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D258-265%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DH.%2BZheng%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BSong%26author%3DG.%2BLiu%26author%3DV.%2BS.%2BBattaglia%26title%3DA%2BComprehensive%2BUnderstanding%2Bof%2BElectrode%2BThickness%2BEffects%2Bon%2Bthe%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformances%2Bof%2BLi-Ion%2BBattery%2BCathodes%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2012.03.161&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2012.03.161&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive understanding of electrode thickness effects on the electrochemical performances of Li-ion battery cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Honghe; Li, Jing; Song, Xiangyun; Liu, Gao; Battaglia, Vincent S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">71</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">258-265</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 and LiFePO4 electrodes of different active material loadings are prepd.  The impact of electrode thickness on the rate capability, energy and power d. and long-term cycling behavior is comparatively investigated.  Peukert coeff. slightly increases with increasing electrode thickness, showing a severe capacity loss at higher rate for thicker electrode.  A power-law relation between the max. working C rate and electrode loading is obtained.  Increase of the specific resistance with increasing electrode thickness is not an important factor responsible for the poor rate performance for thicker electrode.  The power-law relationship is typical for a diffusion-related system, indicating that Li ion diffusion within the electrode is the rate-detg. step for the discharge process.  As the result of the deterioration of rate capability, enhancing energy d. through increasing electrode thickness is accompanied by a significant loss of power d.  Long-term cycling performance also deteriorates, which is attributed to the high internal resistance and poor mech. integrity of thicker electrode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C1CC14764A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhancing the Performances of Li-Ion Batteries by Carbon-Coating: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>48</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1201</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1217</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C1CC14764A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D48%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D1201-1217%26journal%3DChem.%2BCommun.%26author%3DH.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BZhou%26title%3DEnhancing%2Bthe%2BPerformances%2Bof%2BLi-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bby%2BCarbon-Coating%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC1CC14764A&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC1CC14764A&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancing the performances of Li-ion batteries by carbon-coating: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Huiqiao; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Communications (Cambridge, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1201-1217</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHCOFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-7345</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With progress of knowledge of electrode materials, it has been found that their surface structures are of great importance to the electrochem. performance of Li-ion batteries.  Carbon coating can effectively increase the electrode cond., improve the surface chem. of the active material, and protect the electrode from direct contact with electrolyte, leading to enhanced cycle life of the batteries.  Carbon coating together with nanotechnol. provides good cond. as well as fast Li-ion diffusion, and thus also results in good rate capabilities.  The recent development of carbon coating techniques in lithium-ion batteries is discussed with detailed examples of typical cathode and anode materials. Elec. vehicles powered by lithium-ion batteries are viewed as a vital green technol. required to meet CO2 emission targets as part of a global effort to tackle climate change.  Pos. electrode (cathode) materials within such batteries are rich in crit. metals-particularly lithium, cobalt, and nickel.  The large-scale mining of such metals, to meet increasing battery demands, poses concerns surrounding material exhaustion in addn. to further environmental, social, and governance (ESG) issues.  In particular, unethical mining practices and political instability within the Democratic Republic of the Congo (the worlds largest cobalt producer) have prompted research into cobalt-low and cobalt-free alternatives.  This review aims to provide a holistic view of lithium-ion cathode development and inform advancements by highlighting the interdependencies across mining, material development, and end-of-life management.  While material sustainability is reported through supply and demand projections, the potential socioenvironmental impacts of lithium-ion battery technol. represent a hugely underresearched area among the aforementioned themes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage. Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comparison of electrochemical performances of olivine NaFePO4 in sodium-ion batteries and olivine LiFePO4 in lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yujie; Xu, Yunhua; Liu, Yihang; Luo, Chao; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-coated olivine NaFePO4 (C-NaFePO4) spherical particles with a uniform diam. of ∼80 nm are obtained by chem. delithiation and subsequent electrochem. sodiation of carbon-coated olivine LiFePO4 (C-LiFePO4), which is synthesized by a solvothermal method.  The C-NaFePO4 electrodes are identical (particle size, particle size distribution, surface coating, and active material loading, etc.) to C-LiFePO4 except that Li ions in C-LiFePO4 are replaced by Na ions, making them ideal for comparison of thermodn. and kinetics between C-NaFePO4 cathode in sodium-ion (Na-ion) batteries and C-LiFePO4 in lithium-ion (Li-ion) batteries.  In this paper, the equil. potentials, reaction resistances, and diffusion coeff. of Na in C-NaFePO4 are systematically investigated by using the galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT), electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV), and compared to those of the well-known LiFePO4 cathodes in Li-ion batteries.  Due to the lower diffusion coeff. of Na-ion and higher contact and charge transfer resistances in NaFePO4 cathodes, the rate performance of C-NaFePO4 in Na-ion batteries is much worse than that of C-LiFePO4 in Li-ion batteries.  However, the cycling stability of C-NaFePO4 is almost comparable to C-LiFePO4 by retaining 90% of its capacity even after 100 charge-discharge cycles at a charge-discharge rate of 0.1 C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001310"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chayambuka, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mulder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Danilov, D. Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review. However, the sharply increased demands/costs and the limited reserves of the two most important metal elements (Li &amp; Co) for LIBs have raised concerns about future development.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) equipped with advanced cobalt-free cathodes show great potential in solving both "lithium panic" and "cobalt panic", and have made remarkable progress in recent years.  In this, we comprehensively summarize the recent advances of high-performance cobalt-free cathode materials for advanced SIBs, systematically analyze the conflicts of structural/electrochem. stability with intrinsic insufficiencies of cobalt-free cathode materials, and extensively discuss the strategies of constructing stable cobalt-free cathode materials by making full use of non-cobalt transition-metal elements and suitable crystal structures, all of which aim to provide insights into the key factors (e.g., phase transformation, particle cracks, crystal defects, lattice distortion, lattice oxygen oxidn., morphol., transition-metal migration/dissoln., and the synergistic effects of composite structures) that can det. the stability of cobalt-free cathode materials, provide guidelines for future research, and stimulate more interest on constructing high-performance cobalt-free cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.mattod.2023.03.020"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Z.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Emerging Characterization Techniques for Delving Polyanion-Type Cathode Materials of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Mater.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress of emerging cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Jun; Li, Xiao; Tang, Kaikai; Wang, Dandan; Long, Mengqi; Gao, Hong; Chen, Weihua; Liu, Chuntai; Liu, Hao; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Chemistry Frontiers</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recently, due to the excessive consumption of fossil energy and the intermittent nature of clean energy resources, electricity storage has attracted great attention from both academia and industry.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy d. have been widely used for portable energy storage systems.  However, limited lithium resources cannot meet the demands for large-scale applications, esp. the grid-scale energy storage.  Owing to the abundant natural resources of sodium and the low price, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become potential substitutes for LIBs.  Cathode materials are key components that det. the overall performance and entire cost of SIBs.  However, the unstable structure and limited electrochem. performance make them difficult to meet the requirement of practical applications.  This review summarizes the recent development of the cathode materials used for SIBs, and the modified strategies applied to improve their electrochem. performance.  Finally, crit. challenges and perspectives to promote the development of SIBs are also presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                 10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.%2BT.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CS00776G&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.3c02546&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CS00776G&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201501527"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5343-5364</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600275"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni Qiao; Bai Ying; Wu Feng; Wu Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703055"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lander, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanionic Insertion Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering of Polyanion Type Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Toward Higher Energy/Power Density</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Huangxu; Xu, Ming; Zhang, Zhian; Lai, Yanqing; Ma, Jianmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The development of high energy/power d. sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is still challenged by the high redox potential of Na/Na+ and large radius of Na+ ions, thus requiring extensive further improvement to, in particular, enhance the capacity and voltage of cathode materials.  Among the various types of cathodes, the polyanion cathodes have emerged as the most pragmatic option due to their outstanding thermostability, unique inductive effect, and flexible structures.  In this Review, a crit. overview of the design principles and engineering strategies of polyanion cathodes that could have a pivotal role in developing high energy/power d. SIBs are presented.  Specifically, the engineering of polyanion cathode materials for higher voltage and specific capacity to increase energy d. is discussed.  The way in which morphol. control, architectural design, and electrode processing have been developed to increase power d. for SIBs is also analyzed.  Finally, the remaining challenges and the future research direction of this field are presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1038/s41598-017-13718-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muruganantham, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chiu, Y.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An Efficient Evaluation of F-doped Polyanion Cathode Materials with Long Cycle Life for Na-Ion Batteries Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Muruganantham Rasu; Chiu Yi-Tang; Liu Wei-Ren; Yang Chun-Chuen; Wang Chin-Wei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">A series of Na3-x V2(PO4-x F x )3 (x = 0, 0.1, 0.15 and 0.3) polyanion cathode materials are synthesized via a sol-gel method.  The optimal doping concentration of F in Na3V2(PO4)3 is 0.15 mol %.  By neutron powder diffraction data, the chemical composition of as-synthesized material is Na2.85V2(PO3.95F0.05)3.  The half-cell of Na2.85V2(PO3.95F0.05)3 cathode exhibits a stable discharge capacity of 103 mAh g(-1) and 93% of capacity retention over 250 cycles without decay at 0.1 A g(-1), which is higher than that of bare Na3V2(PO4)3 (98 mAh g(-1)).  The high rate capability of Na2.85V2(PO3.95F0.05)3 is also dramatically enhanced via increase the conductivity of host material by F-doping.  Moreover, the symmetrical Na-ion full-cell is fabricated using Na2.85V2(PO3.95F0.05)3 as cathode and anode materials.  It is achieved that the good reversibility and superior cycling stability about 98% of capacity retention with ~100% of coulombic efficiency at 1.0 A g(-1) throughout 1000 cycles.  These results demonstrate that the optimal amount of Na2.85V2(PO3.95F0.05)3 is a distinctive potential candidate for excellent long-term cyclic stability with high rate low-cost energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/c2jm35293a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goñi, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gil de Muro, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubiak, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lezama, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Voltage Cathode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries of General Formula Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3-2x</sub></span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Different samples of the Na-V fluorophosphate cathodic materials have been synthesized via the hydrothermal method, varying the type and content of C used in the synthesis.  Structural characterization of the composites was performed by powder x-ray diffraction.  Magnetic susceptibility measurements and EPR (ESR) polycryst. spectra indicate that some of the samples exhibit V3+/V4+ mixed valence, Na3V2O2x(PO4)2F3-2x where 0 ≤ x &lt; 1.  The morphol. of the materials was analyzed by TEM.  A correlation between the type and content of C with the electrochem. behavior of the different samples was established.  Electrochem. measurements conducted using Swagelok-type cells showed 2 voltage plateaus at 3.6 and 4.1 V vs. Na/Na+.  The best performing sample, which comprised a moderate percentage of electrochem. grade C as additive, exhibited specific capacity values of ∼100 mA-h g-1 at 1C (≈80% of theor. specific capacity).  Cyclability tests at 1C proved good reversibility of the material that maintained 98% of initial specific capacity for 30 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In the search for new pos.-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, recent research has focused on nanostructured lithium transition-metal phosphates that exhibit desirable properties such as high energy storage capacity combined with electrochem. stability.  Only one member of this class-the olivine LiFePO4 (ref. 3)-has risen to prominence so far, owing to its other characteristics, which include low cost, low environmental impact and safety.  These are crit. for large-capacity systems such as plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles.  Nonetheless, olivine has some inherent shortcomings, including 1-dimensional lithium-ion transport and a two-phase redox reaction that together limit the mobility of the phase boundary. However, the low intrinsic electronic cond. of Na2FePO4F suppresses its practical applications.  Herein, ion doping strategy is employed to improve the electrochem. performance of Na2FePO4F as the cathode of sodium ion batteries. The obtained results show a decrease of the band gap energy from 2.19 eV to 1.36 eV with the GGA + U approxn. and from 2.80 eV to 1.93 eV with the HSE hybrid functional, by doping 50% of the Fe site in the Na2FePO4F lattice with Mn. M.; Islam, M. S.; Nazar, L. F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Na-ion battery is currently the focus of much research interest due to its cost advantages and the relative abundance of sodium as compared to lithium.  Olivine NaMPO4 (M = Fe, Fe0.5Mn0.5, Mn) and layered Na2FePO4F are interesting materials that have been reported recently as attractive pos. electrodes.  Here, we report their Na-ion conduction behavior and intrinsic defect properties using atomistic simulation methods.  In the olivines, Na ion migration is essentially restricted to the [010] direction along a curved trajectory, similar to that of LiMPO4, but with a lower migration energy (0.3 eV).  However, Na/M antisite defects are also predicted to have a lower formation energy: the higher probability of tunnel occupation with a relatively immobile M2+ cation - along with a greater vol. change on redox cycling - contributes to the poor electrochem. performance of the Na-olivine.  Na+ ion conduction in Na2FePO4F is predicted to be two-dimensional (2D) in the interlayer plane with a similar low activation energy.  The antisite formation energy is slightly higher; furthermore, antisite occupation would not be predicted to impede transport significantly owing to the 2D pathway.  This factor, along with the much lower vol. change on redox cycling, is undoubtedly responsible for the better electrochem. performance of the layered structure.  Where vol. change and structural effects do not incur impediments, Na-ion materials may present significant advantages over their Li counterparts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2011.08.038"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kawabe, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kajiyama, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fukuhara, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Inamasu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okuyama, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakai, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis and Electrode Performance of Carbon Coated Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochem. The carbon-coated Na2FePO4F sample (1.3 wt% carbon) delivers initial discharge capacity of 110 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1/20 C (6.2 mA g-1) with well-defined voltage plateaus at 3.06 and 2.91 V vs. Na metal.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Monoclinic α-Na2FePO4F with Strong Antisite Disorder and Enhanced Na+ Diffusion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kirsanova, Maria A.; Akmaev, Alexey S.; Aksyonov, Dmitry A.; Ryazantsev, Sergey V.; Nikitina, Victoria A.; Filimonov, Dmitry S.; Avdeev, Maxim; Abakumov, Artem M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new monoclinic α-polymorph of the Na2FePO4F fluoride-phosphate has been directly synthesized via a hydrothermal method for application in metal-ion batteries.  The crystal structure of the as-prepd. α-Na2FePO4F studied with powder X-ray and neutron diffraction (P21/c, a = 13.6753(10) Å, b = 5.2503(2) Å, c = 13.7202(8) Å, β = 120.230(4)°) demonstrates strong antisite disorder between the Na and Fe atoms.  As revealed with DFT-based calcns., α-Na2FePO4F has low migration barriers for Na+ along the main pathway parallel to the b axis, and an addnl. diffusion bypass allowing the Na+ cations to go around the Na/Fe antisite defects.  These results corroborate with the extremely high exptl. Na-ion diffusion coeff. of (1-5)·10-11 cm2·s-1, which is 2 orders of magnitude higher than that for the orthorhombic β-polymorph ((5-10)·10-13 cm2·s-1).  Being tested as a cathode material in Na- and Li-ion battery cells, monoclinic α-Na2FePO4F exhibits a reversible specific capacity of 90 and 80 mAh g-1, resp.  Hydrothermal synthesis yielded a new monoclinic α-Na2FePO4F fluoride-phosphate with strong positional disorder between the Na and Fe atoms.  Despite the antisite disorder, the material demonstrates high Na-ion diffusivity most probably attributed to a lower migration barrier along the main diffusion pathway and the ability of Na+ cations to migrate around the Na/Fe antisite defects.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1021/ic402513d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Avdeev, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ling, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2FePO4F is a promising cathode material for Na-ion batteries owing to its relatively high discharge voltage and excellent cycling performance.  Now, the long- and short-range structural evolution of Na2FePO4F during cycling is studied by in situ high-energy X-ray diffraction (XRD), ex situ solid-state NMR (NMR), and first-principles DFT calcns.  DFT calcns. suggest that the intermediate phase, Na1.5FePO4F, adopts the space group of P21/c, which is a subgroup (P21/b11, No. 14) of Pbcn (No. 60), the space group of the starting phase, Na2FePO4F, and this space group provides a good fit to the exptl. However, for the first time, we have obsd. antisite defects in LiFePO4 prepd. through a sol-gel process at a higher temp. of 700°C.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Explaining Performance-Limiting Mechanisms in Fluorophosphate Na-Ion Battery Cathodes through Inactive Transition-Metal Mixing and First-Principles Mobility Calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Matts, Ian L.; Dacek, Stephen; Pietrzak, Tomasz K.; Malik, Rahul; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fluorophosphate cathodes are currently one of the most promising polyanionic Na-ion battery cathodes and exhibit specific energies not far below oxide cathodes.  To further improve fluorophosphate cathodes, their capacity must be increased, which might be possible since some Na remains unextd. in these cathodes during cycling.  The authors attempt to answer the question of what specific mechanism limits fluorophosphate cathode capacity, which could stem from either redox-limiting or site-limiting behavior.  This paper reports the synthesis, electrochem. characterization, and computational examn. of Na3GaV(PO4)2F3.  This test system, which was designed explicitly for uncovering the limiting factors in these structures, exhibits reversible insertion of Na+ and redox activity for V2+ through V5+ during electrochem. cycling, indicating that fluorophosphate cathodes are not fundamentally redox-limited and must be site-limited.  First-principles calcns. indicate that large diffusion barriers at high sodiations impose a kinetic limit on Na+ insertion in fluorophosphate cathodes, but further study is needed to det. capacity limits on Na+ extn.  From the authors' combined results the authors also propose possible routes to improve future fluorophosphate cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.2c00986"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Influence Mechanism of Mg<sup>2+</sup> Doping on Electrochemical Properties of LiFePO<sub>4</sub> Cathode Materials</span>. <i>ACS Appl. X-ray diffraction and refinement data suggested that an appropriate doping amt. of Mg2+ can reduce the cell vol. of LFP, shorten the Fe-O and P-O bonds, and elongate the Li-O bond, thereby facilitating the diffusion of Li+.  XPS test results revealed that Mg2+ doping prevents the formation of Li-Fe antisite defects while also promoting the formation of Fe2P, thereby improving the electronic cond. of the LFP.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered transition metal oxides used as cathodes in modern metal-ion batteries are prone to surface reconstruction, which is a key reason for electrochem. performance degrdn.  Despite extensive research on this class of materials the mechanism of surface reconstruction is still not fully clear.  In this work, we use d. functional theory to study the influence of antisite defect pairs on the surface structure and energetics in pristine oxide materials: LiNiO2, LiCoO2, NaNiO2, and NaCoO2.  Wullf constructions were built to predict thermodynamically favorable surfaces and their orientation, which are in agreement with expts.  The energetics and structure were studied and compared for surface and bulk antisite defect pairs, which can be accompanied by transition metal charge disproportionation to +2 and +4, with the latter corresponding to the formation of a small hole polaron.  Individual antisite complexes at the surface are found to be addnl. stabilized opposite to those in the bulk, while no excess energy is released for the complete antisite-based surface reconstruction.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Controlling Li/Ni disordering has always been a priority in designing Co-free layered oxide cathodes.  The Li/Ni disordering is an at.-scale structural defect that has been extensively studied by macroscopic statistical characterizations.  Significantly less is known about its microstructure in the layered structure and correlations with electrochem. performance.  In this work, combining multiscale structural characterizations, it is found that Li/Ni disordering surprisingly takes various microstructural forms in Co-free layered cathodes.  Li/Ni disordering at insufficient calcination temp. tends to manifest as localized rock salt nanodomain in the layered structure.  However, an excessively high calcination temp. causes Li/Ni disordering as a massive rock salt phase inside the particles.  Only at an appropriate calcination temp., the Li/Ni disordering is present in the layered structure as the form of well-known anti-site defects.  These microstructural differences lead to widely varying electrochem. performance, and an in-depth structure-performance connection is built.  Besides, the above results also reflect that more careful and precise control of exptl. conditions is required to synthesize Co-free layered cathodes.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Robust Surface Reconstruction Induced by Subsurface Ni/Li Antisites in Ni-Rich Cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xinyan; Gao, Ang; Tang, Zhexin; Meng, Fanqi; Shang, Tongtong; Guo, Shengnan; Ding, Jiarun; Luo, Yanhong; Xiao, Dongdong; Wang, Xuefeng; Su, Dong; Zhang, Qinghua; Gu, Lin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Loss of active materials is a crit. problem of layered oxide cathodes for lithium-ion batteries and undermines their long-term electrochem. performance.  However, the at.-scale outward migration mechanism of transition metals and oxygen remains elusive due to a highly localized environment at surface.  Here, the robust surface reconstruction of LiNi0.8Mn0.1Co0.1O2 (NMC811) induced by artificially introduced Ni/Li antisites is reported.  Using scanning transmission electron microscopy, the outward co-migration process of nickel and oxygen ions is directly revealed at the at. scale, finally resulting in a stable surface structure.  The robust nature of this surface structure originates from the strong linear superexchange interaction between subsurface NiLi and surface Ni as supported by first-principles calcns.  An idealized subsurface structure with 13 NiLi is designed to suppress the outward migration of transition metal and oxygen ions and provide a universal lattice-coherent surface protection strategy for layered lithium transition metal oxide cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-022-4532-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tuning Li-Excess to Optimize Ni/Li Exchange and Improve Stability of Structure in LiNi<sub>0.&nbsp;<i>8</i></sub><i>C<sub>0.1</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material for Lithium-Lon Batteries</i></span>. <i>Nano Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">8962</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8971</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tuning Li-excess to optimize Ni/Li exchange and improve stability of structure in LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn 0.1O2 cathode material for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Fangya; Xie, Yongfan; Zhang, Youxiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ni/Li exchange is a detrimental effect on electrochem. performances for high-Ni cathode materials (LiNixCoyMnzO2, x ≥ 0.6).  Adjusting Li-excess degree has been proved to be an effective way to optimize Ni/Li exchange in the materials.  However, until now, how the Ni/Li exchange and thus the structural properties is affected by the Li-excess has not been understood and clearly elucidated in the literature.  Herein, a feasible strategy is utilized to optimize Ni/Li exchange and the amt. of anti-Li+ in LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn 0.1O2 by mixing Ni0.8Co0.1Mn0.1(OH)2 precursor with different amts. of lithium sources during lithiation.  It was found that morphol. and phase stability of the material can be tuned with moderate excessive lithium.  With 10% Li-excess, LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn 0.1O2 exhibits an initial discharge capacity of 211.5 mAh·g-1 at 0.1 C and maintains 93.3% of its initial capacity after 100 cycles at 1 C.  Different technologies were used to characterize the materials and it shows that the formation of broader Li slab space, decreased anti-Ni2+ in Li layer, and gradient distribution of Ni3+ in the surface is contributed to moderate Li-excess in the materials.  Broader Li slab space facilitates diffusion of Li+, decreased antisite-Ni2+ and gradient distribution of Ni3+ in materials surfaces optimizes the Ni/Li exchange.  Based on these results, we thus believe that it is the moderate Li-excess in material that optimized the electrochem. performance of high-Ni cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA05062F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Molecular Dynamics Simulations on Lithium Diffusion in LiFePO<sub>4</sub>: the Effect of Anti-Site Defects</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Molecular dynamics simulations on lithium diffusion in LiFePO4: the effect of anti-site defects</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Boyang; Tao, Guohua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As a promising cathode material for rechargeable lithium ion batteries, lithium iron phosphate (LiFePO4) is prevented from further implementations due to its low ionic and electronic cond.  Anti-site defects were reported earlier to be responsible for the slow lithium ion diffusion.  However, the underlying mechanism is controversial.  In this work we perform mol. dynamics simulations to investigate the real time diffusion dynamics of lithium ions in LiFePO4 contg. Li-Fe anti-site defects.  Our results indicate that the anti-site defects indeed slow down Li-ion diffusion in [010] channels.  However as the concn. of anti-site defects increases, Li-ion diffusion becomes more isotropic.  A local structural transition from the solid phase to a liq.-like solid soln. in LiFePO4 with anti-site defects is obsd. around the defect concn. of 8%, which coincides with the dynamical transition from the anisotropic 1D diffusion of Li ions to the isotropic 2D or 3D diffusion.  At high temps., the anti-site defects could even enhance Li diffusion.  The defect distribution also plays an important role in modulating Li diffusion.  The recombination of the anti-site defect and the inter-channel crossing are obsd. for the edge-shared defect model.  Furthermore the aggregation of defects could induce quite different local environments, resulting in heterogeneous diffusion, which may reconcile the theor. predictions on the Li diffusion coeff. with the exptl. measurements.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1126/science.1246432"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Urban, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hautier, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unlocking the Potential of Cation-Disordered Oxides for Rechargeable Lithium Batteries</span>. <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>343</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">519</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">522</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nearly all high-energy d. cathodes for rechargeable lithium batteries are well-ordered materials in which lithium and other cations occupy distinct sites.  Cation-disordered materials are generally disregarded as cathodes because lithium diffusion tends to be limited by their structures.  The performance of Li1.211Mo0.467Cr0.3O2 shows that lithium diffusion can be facile in disordered materials.  Using ab initio computations, it is demonstrated that this unexpected behavior is due to percolation of a certain type of active diffusion channels in disordered Li-excess materials.  A unified understanding of high performance in both layered and Li-excess materials may enable the design of disordered-electrode materials with high capacity and high energy d.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b03746"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Karakulina, O.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Antisite Disorder and Bond Valence Compensation in Li2FePO4F Cathode for Li-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Karakulina, Olesia M.; Khasanova, Nellie R.; Drozhzhin, Oleg A.; Tsirlin, Alexander A.; Hadermann, Joke; Antipov, Evgeny V.; Abakumov, Artem M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7578-7581</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report on a remarkable Li/Fe antisite disorder upon electrochem. cycling of the Li2FePO4F cathode for Li-ion batteries.  As reflected by electron diffraction tomog. structure anal., up to 30-40% of Fe is located in the Li positions after 10 charge-discharge cycles at room temp. and at 75 oC.  This massive antisite disorder is attributed to the peculiarities of the three-dimensional framework structure of Li2FePO4F: the presence of oxygen atoms linked exclusively to Li and P atoms ("dangling" P-O bonds) and oxygen atoms at the common edges of the edge-sharing FeO4F2 octahedra.  Li deintercalation results in underbonding of the O atoms at the "dangling" P-O bonds that is compensated by a migration of the Fe3+ cation to the Li sites.  At the same time, the Li cations migrating to the Fe sites relieve overbonding of the O atoms at the common edge of the FeO4F2 octahedra.  Olivine-structured LiFePO4 having no such structural features, does not demonstrate antisite disorder being cycled even at 100 oC.  Bond valence compensation for the oxygen atoms in the "dangling" bonds of polyanion groups is suggested to be a triggering factor for antisite disorder in polyanion cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.9b00033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ni/Li Disordering in Layered Transition Metal Oxide: Electrochemical Impact, Origin, and Control</span>. <i>Acc.
